  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1c382539-b473-44b7-85d5-be358323dd54'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9215398916791401199">In this volume, which reaffirms the uncompromising brilliance of his mind, Cioran strips the human condition down to its most basic components, birth and death, suggesting that disaster lies not in the prospect of death but in the fact of birth, "that laughable accident." In the lucid, aphoristic style that characterizes his work, Cioran writes of time and death, God and r</span>
  <span id="freeText9215398916791401199" style="display:none">In this volume, which reaffirms the uncompromising brilliance of his mind, Cioran strips the human condition down to its most basic components, birth and death, suggesting that disaster lies not in the prospect of death but in the fact of birth, "that laughable accident." In the lucid, aphoristic style that characterizes his work, Cioran writes of time and death, God and religion, suicide and suffering, and the temptation to silence. In all his writing, Cioran cuts to the heart of the human experience.</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I would cautiously. It's a good read for those who want to better understand a relatively extreme perspective that doesn't seem open to other perspect<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I would cautiously. It's a good read for those who want to better understand a relatively extreme perspective that doesn't seem open to other perspectives. That is a perspective in itself. Not for the meek.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7817621922294410123"><b> Life Inside the Bubble </b> <br/><br/>I feel entitled to interpret and respond to Cioran’s aphoristic mode with some of the same chiastic development (even if not nearly as witty):<br/><br/>Homo sapiens, uniquely in the animal kingdom, lives in a bubble of language. He (and she) is so immured in this bubble that language and experience are inextricably confounded. The consequence of this involuntary universal peonage is that everyone substitutes things with words and responds to words as if they were things. It’s the </span>
  <span id="freeText7817621922294410123" style="display:none"><b> Life Inside the Bubble </b> <br /><br />I feel entitled to interpret and respond to Cioran’s aphoristic mode with some of the same chiastic development (even if not nearly as witty):<br /><br />Homo sapiens, uniquely in the animal kingdom, lives in a bubble of language. He (and she) is so immured in this bubble that language and experience are inextricably confounded. The consequence of this involuntary universal peonage is that everyone substitutes things with words and responds to words as if they were things. It’s the cost of living in the bubble.<br /><br />Reality is what happens. Everything else is literature.<br /><br />Literature is an evolved form of language. It is constituted by an ideal philosophy, and ideal religion, and an ideal politics. Or at least as ideal as can be reached by Homo sapiens.<br /><br />The philosophy of literature is empirically grounded on observation: human beings are the only story-telling animal. Other sentient beings use gestures, sounds, words, phrases, even sentences to communicate with each other. Only people connect words in complex creative ways. This is a blessing and a curse. It makes life inside the language bubble bearable but more or less isolates story-tellers from experience since the stories they tell create their own experience. No one has ever found a way to untangle the two (the attempt is the failed science of epistemology).<br /><br />To compensate for the consequences of entrapment inside the bubble human beings have invented a religion of language (and a language of religion) that tells the story (actually many stories) of what exists outside the bubble. This of course is paradoxical since that which is beyond the bubble is reality, which as soon as it is brought inside the bubble becomes literature. Prompted by this contradiction, some people declare their language about things outside the bubble to be sacred, thus making life inside the bubble toxic. These people are idolatrous and call those who are not idolaters: atheists, agnostics, non-conformists, dreamers, and sometimes artists, by which they mean useless.<br /><br />Those who recognise the existence of the bubble and its implications strive to keep story-telling free from such ossification. Feeling in need of support in a hostile world, they too have succumbed to the religious impulse but in a very different way. Their alternative religion is a kind of ethical politics which allows any story to be told and heard. They make no claims to knowing what is outside the bubble or approaching closer to it by working hard at story-telling within the bubble. Their life consists of the unrestricted exchange of words in unusual and unexpected combinations. They often allude to what they imagine might be outside the bubble but remain interested in the imaginations of others. From this they derive pleasure from which many other inhabitants of the bubble take offence.<br /><br />Typically, those who take offence, whether religious or not, claim that the imaginative new stories are not reflections of reality and should be ignored or even banned as dangerous. This, of course, is a story of limited imagination (and probably a restricted vocabulary; they tend to occur together). Such stories have little weight unless accompanied by violence. Violence - physical, psychological, and spiritual - is the only effective method which allows reality to enter the bubble. Violence shatters the bubble completely. This those offended perceive as satisfying.<br /><br />Literature has no defense against violence. The bubble is an aberration, as fragile, ephemeral, and temporary as the language upon which it is based.<br /><br />In the end violence, that is to say, reality prevails.<br /><br />Of course Cioran is a laconic genius; so he summarises the situation much more compactly:<i> "As long as you live on this side of the terrible, you will find words to express it; once you know it from inside, you will no longer find a single one." </i>It is difficult to cope with such terseness, even among the literate. Such is the character of good literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7817621922294410123" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1556610712201084088">This book published as an essay is a surprising collection of relentless thoughts put down by Emil Cioran over life. <br/><br/>Cioran considers life not as an ontological abstraction, but a situation in which you have to go out, meet people in the street, have a look at your prospects, whether they are your own books in a library or job offers in your corner agency... You would feel quite the same at reading a notepad teeming with wild thoughts all over the place.<br/><br/><br/>Is Cioran sportsmanlike or is he putting a</span>
  <span id="freeText1556610712201084088" style="display:none">This book published as an essay is a surprising collection of relentless thoughts put down by Emil Cioran over life. <br /><br />Cioran considers life not as an ontological abstraction, but a situation in which you have to go out, meet people in the street, have a look at your prospects, whether they are your own books in a library or job offers in your corner agency... You would feel quite the same at reading a notepad teeming with wild thoughts all over the place.<br /><br /><br />Is Cioran sportsmanlike or is he putting a sham sometimes? In the end, does that matter? Emil Cioran is a man of contradictions, yet, highly consistent in his contradictions. He writes every one of them in earnest.<br /><br /><br />Here are some thoughts to mull over. Please help yourself :<br /><br /><br /><b>Some have misfortunes, others have obsessions. Who are most to be pitied?</b><br />'Certains ont des malheurs d'autres des obsessions. Lesquels sont les plus à plaindre ?'<br /><br /><br /><b>As arts are going deeper into an impasse, artists multiply. This abnormality isn't one if one thinks that art, on the decline, has become impossible and easy at the same time.</b><br />'A mesure que l'art s'enfonce dans l'impasse, les artistes se multiplient. Cette anomalie cesse d'en être une, si l'on songe que l'art, en voie d'épuisement, est devenu à la fois impossible et facile.'<br /><br /><br /><b>All these nations were great, because they had great prejudices. They no longer have. Are they nations still? Scattered crowds, at best.</b><br />'Tous ces peuples étaient grands, parce qu'ils avaient de grands préjugés. Ils n'en ont plus. Sont-ils encore des nations? Tout au plus des foules désagrégées.'<br /><br /><br /><b>To get up, wash yourself, then wait for some kind of blues or angst. I would give the whole universe and Shakespeare for a bit of ataraxia.</b><br />'Se lever, faire sa toilette et puis attendre quelque variété imprévue de cafard ou d'effroi. Je donnerais l'univers entier et tout Shakespeare pour un brin d'ataraxie.'<br /><br /><br /><b>If attachment is an evil, the cause lies in the disgrace of being born, because being born is being attached. Detachment should consist in suppressing this disgrace, the most severe and unbearable of all.</b><br />'Si l'attachement est un mal, il faut en chercher la cause dans le scandale de la naissance, car naître c'est s'attacher. Le détachement devrait donc s'appliquer à faire disparaître les traces de ce scandale, le plus grave et le plus intolérable de tous.'<br /><br /><br /><b>When you refuse to be lyrical, to fill a page becomes a trial : why bother writing exactly what you meant in the first place?</b><br />'Quand on se refuse au lyrisme, noircir une page devient une épreuve: à quoi bon écrire pour dire exactement ce qu'on avait à dire ?'</span>

<div id="review_35645891" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35645891" />
    <a title="M. D.  Hudson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1478575-m-d-hudson"><img alt="M. D.  Hudson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593964179p2/1478575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35645891?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M. D.  Hudson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M. D.  Hudson" href="/user/show/1478575-m-d-hudson">M. D.  Hudson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35645891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1994778420059625048">I do not read philosophy generally, because it confuses me almost immediately given that I have no capacity whatsoever for abstractions. But I can do E. M. Cioran. Cioran was born in Romania in 1911, spent most of his working life in France, mulling over suicide and death while living to a ripe old age. Much of Cioran’s work is in the form of aphorisms or maxims, and are therefore accessible (I re-read this book recently; I return to his work every few years or so). He is the most relentlessly p</span>
  <span id="freeText1994778420059625048" style="display:none">I do not read philosophy generally, because it confuses me almost immediately given that I have no capacity whatsoever for abstractions. But I can do E. M. Cioran. Cioran was born in Romania in 1911, spent most of his working life in France, mulling over suicide and death while living to a ripe old age. Much of Cioran’s work is in the form of aphorisms or maxims, and are therefore accessible (I re-read this book recently; I return to his work every few years or so). He is the most relentlessly pessimistic human being that has ever lived, which you might have guessed from the title, and this makes for some really, really fun reading. Sometimes he makes a lot of sense. Below are a few of my favorites. Several mention poetry, which is not one of his chief preoccupations, and yet he says interesting things about it: <br /><br /> Every misanthrope, however sincere, at times reminds me of that old poet, bedridden and utterly forgotten, who in a rage with his contemporaries declared he would receive none of them. His wife, out of charity, would ring at the door from time to time…<br /><br /> As art sinks into paralysis, artists multiply. This anomaly ceases to be one if we realize that art, on its way to exhaustion, has become both impossible and easy. <br /><br /> All these poems where it is merely the Poem that is in question – a whole poetry with no other substance than itself! What would we say of a prayer whose object was religion?<br /><br /> When I torment myself a little too much for not working, I tell myself that I might just as well be dead and that then I would be working still less…<br /><br /> Only one thing matters: learning to be the loser.<br /><br /> A writer has left his mark on us not because we have read him a great deal but because we have thought of him more than is warranted. I have not frequented Baudelaire or Pascal particularly, but I have not stopped thinking of their miseries, which have accompanied me everywhere as faithfully as my own. <br /><br /> Only what proceeds from emotion or from cynicism is real. All the rest is “talent.”<br /><br /> Sometimes I wish I were a cannibal – less for the pleasure of eating someone than for the pleasure of vomiting him.<br /><br />That last one about the cannibal cracked me up so much in the middle of the night that my laughter scared the cats off the bed. By this I mean that pessimism does get to a point where it is hilarious. And yet, Cioran is not a clown. Nope. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1994778420059625048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21737486" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21737486" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/832637-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1426881597p2/832637.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21737486?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/832637-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">pessimists</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21737486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4312619160373742272">"When every man has realized that his birth is a defeat, existence, endurable at last, will seem like the day after a surrender, like the relief and the repose of the conquered."<br/><br/><u>The Trouble With Being Born</u> is a collection of aphorisms mostly about (but not limited to) the horrors unleashed on us by birth. Cioran argues that perhaps existence is exile and oblivion itself is salvation.<br/><br/>However, Cioran's wit and (dare I say) charm prevent this book from becoming what would otherwise be an overwhelmi</span>
  <span id="freeText4312619160373742272" style="display:none">"When every man has realized that his birth is a defeat, existence, endurable at last, will seem like the day after a surrender, like the relief and the repose of the conquered."<br /><br /><u>The Trouble With Being Born</u> is a collection of aphorisms mostly about (but not limited to) the horrors unleashed on us by birth. Cioran argues that perhaps existence is exile and oblivion itself is salvation.<br /><br />However, Cioran's wit and (dare I say) charm prevent this book from becoming what would otherwise be an overwhelmingly crushing read. The compact and concise form of the aphorism fits well with his philosophy; since a minimalist, ascetic lifestyle seems to be the only way to peel back the layers of existence in order to uncover the Truth, long, involved treatises are highly unnecessary.<br /><br /><u>The Trouble With Being Born</u> is a highly readable reflection on the meaninglessness of existence, a concentrated dose of pessimism that nudges the curious in the direction of simultaneous salvation and damnation all at once. <br /><br />"Explosive force of any mortification. Every vanquished desire affords us power. We have the more hold over this world the further we withdraw from it, the less we adhere to it. Renunciation confers an infinite power."</span>
  <a data-text-id="4312619160373742272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3203614143" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3203614143" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/100961745-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601796146p2/100961745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3203614143?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/100961745-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781611457407" href="/book/show/15094463-the-trouble-with-being-born">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=1900s-ce">1900s-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=cioran">cioran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100961745-william?shelf=counter-philosophy">counter-philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3203614143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10712555841795012992">Emil Cioran was the master of performing philosophy as poetry. In this volume, which is composed almost entirely of aphorisms and short pieces of creative writing, he touches on the existential loathing borne from being torn from the preconscious. Significant is the idea of being stunted in ones attempts to vocalize the totality of one's angst, muted by the shortcomings of language. I can already tell I'll be returning to this one at dismal midnight hours...</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3214059096" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3214059096" />
    <a title="S̶e̶a̶n̶" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40012758-s-e-a-n"><img alt="S̶e̶a̶n̶" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566235680p2/40012758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3214059096?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S̶e̶a̶n̶" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S̶e̶a̶n̶" href="/user/show/40012758-s-e-a-n">S̶e̶a̶n̶</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670732623" href="/book/show/29725673-the-trouble-with-being-born">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=somewhere-else">somewhere-else</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=philophastery">philophastery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=aphoristic">aphoristic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3214059096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10390370905638462904"><blockquote><i>That faint light in each of us which dates back to before our birth, to before all births, is what must be protected if we want to rejoin that remote glory from which we shall never know why we are separated.</i> [157]</blockquote>Originally published in 1973 when E. M. Cioran was 62 years old, the book is divided into 12 sections. Much of the content is presented in an aphoristic form, although Cioran intersperses other types of entries throughout, to include anecdotes, personal notes, and diatribes. Cioran con</span>
  <span id="freeText10390370905638462904" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>That faint light in each of us which dates back to before our birth, to before all births, is what must be protected if we want to rejoin that remote glory from which we shall never know why we are separated.</i> [157]</blockquote>Originally published in 1973 when E. M. Cioran was 62 years old, the book is divided into 12 sections. Much of the content is presented in an aphoristic form, although Cioran intersperses other types of entries throughout, to include anecdotes, personal notes, and diatribes. Cioran considered himself near or at the end of his career as he was writing the book (per one of the entries). So, if you’re going to read one of his books perhaps this is the best one to read, as it contains his reflections over a lifetime of work, observations, and ruminations. <br /><br />Now to the underlying theme of the book. A significant part of the problem with being born is that we’re not given the choice to opt out. If asked, certainly some of us would have said no. And yet here we are. So what to do about it. If one is a truth-seeker, one can take a degree of comfort in the fact that Cioran has read all the old ‘sages’ and found no so-called ‘truth’ to set him free. Cioran himself makes no claim to being a wise man or sage. As he wrote this book, he was merely an intelligent older man who had managed to clear away many of life’s distractions. Think of his process as making notes while sitting in a metal folding chair at one end of an uncluttered hallway of indeterminate length, at the other end of which crouches his death somewhere in the shadows. Because the fact is that when one does manage to clear away life’s distractions (e.g., desire, ambition, religious faith, relationships, art, etc.), what one is left facing is the inevitability of one’s own death. There it stands in all of its stark clarity. A logical step at this point would be to simply take one’s own life, and yet this is tricky to pull off when in a balanced, analytical state of mind. Human beings have this tendency toward self-preservation, and it persists even as we age and observe our own bodies slowly deteriorate in preparation for the final act. So what are we supposed to do while waiting around. Who knows. This book certainly won’t provide answers, nor will any other book. Perhaps the best thing to do is to help others who are about to die. Be with them, make them comfortable, as they prepare to return to where they were before they were born, i.e., ‘that remote glory from which we shall never know why we are separated’.<br /><br />This is not a depressing book. Even at its most pessimistic, Cioran’s writing holds charm. He maintains a level of intimacy throughout, lacing his observations with wit and humor (typically of the dark, biting variety). If your outlook is already of a certain disposition akin to Cioran’s, this book will offer affirmation. But if your pessimism doesn’t require affirmation (and really, why should it), then you’re probably better off just taking a walk.<blockquote><i>Walking in a forest between two hedges of ferns transfigured by autumn—that is a</i> triumph<i>. What are ovations and applause beside it?</i></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="10390370905638462904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12174741573351079277">I don't have time to be depressed anymore but this book certainly helps!<br/><br/>I think if I had read this book in high school it would've been my favourite book of all time. I was legitimately depressed and I really would have felt what Cioran is saying in my bones. If I had read it in university I would've loved it. Its so beautifully written and while I wasn't as depressed, I would have had the time to process it and peace of mind to quietly contemplate it. I have in fact read it now as an adult with</span>
  <span id="freeText12174741573351079277" style="display:none">I don't have time to be depressed anymore but this book certainly helps!<br /><br />I think if I had read this book in high school it would've been my favourite book of all time. I was legitimately depressed and I really would have felt what Cioran is saying in my bones. If I had read it in university I would've loved it. Its so beautifully written and while I wasn't as depressed, I would have had the time to process it and peace of mind to quietly contemplate it. I have in fact read it now as an adult with a job and I honestly just flipped through it going "that's nice" "that's a nice way of saying it" "huh, neat turn of phrase" "so true" and really I was just trying to get through it.<br /><br />What I'm trying to say is I finished this book more disappointed in myself and the sadness I found in reading this book is that my life is so soul crushingly uncomfortable I can't even read a sad book and feel sad without feeling like I'm wasting time and that I should be working.<br /><br />The true depression is the friends we made on the way.</span>

<div id="review_2777354488" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2777354488" />
    <a title="Tom Quinn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31467977-tom-quinn"><img alt="Tom Quinn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574458226p2/31467977.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2777354488?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Quinn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Quinn" href="/user/show/31467977-tom-quinn">Tom Quinn</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2777354488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8898174009172817180">It's like <i>Meditations</i> for the depressed.<br/><br/>I mean, I'm a cynical pessimist with depressive tendencies myself, but this is ludicrously over-the-top. It's nearly to the point of comedy. Certainly something can be said of the Buddhist exercise of meditating on the dead—<i>if you end up appreciating life as a result</i>. This is so relentlessly pessimistic that it's a challenge to overcome the author's bleak inertia. For example:<br/><br/><i>Man gives off a special odor: of all the animals, he alone smells of the corpse</i>. </span>
  <span id="freeText8898174009172817180" style="display:none">It's like <i>Meditations</i> for the depressed.<br /><br />I mean, I'm a cynical pessimist with depressive tendencies myself, but this is ludicrously over-the-top. It's nearly to the point of comedy. Certainly something can be said of the Buddhist exercise of meditating on the dead—<i>if you end up appreciating life as a result</i>. This is so relentlessly pessimistic that it's a challenge to overcome the author's bleak inertia. For example:<br /><br /><i>Man gives off a special odor: of all the animals, he alone smells of the corpse</i>. (208)<br /><br />or<br /><br /><i>What I knew at sixty, I knew as well at twenty. Forty years of a long, a superfluous, labor of verification</i>. (7)<br /><br />(And it goes on like this.)<br /><br />4 stars out of 5. There are some good aphorisms and some insightfully witty statements in here, but it's so strung out for so long that as a whole it's unpleasant and overall uncomfortable to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8898174009172817180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eadweard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eadweard" href="/user/show/16652983-eadweard">Eadweard</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13361700-the-trouble-with-being-born">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16652983-eadweard?shelf=non-fiction-read">non-fiction-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16652983-eadweard?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16652983-eadweard?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer986666133" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5751718442548539329">Existence would be a quite impracticable enterprise if we stopped granting importance to what has none.<br/>----<br/><br/>For the victim of anxiety, there is no difference between success and fiasco. His reaction to the one is the same as to the other: both trouble him equally.<br/>----<br/><br/><br/>I was alone in that cemetery overlooking the village when a pregnant woman came in. I left at once, in order not to look at this corpse-bearer at close range, nor to ruminate upon the contrast between an aggressive womb and the time-</span>
  <span id="freeText5751718442548539329" style="display:none">Existence would be a quite impracticable enterprise if we stopped granting importance to what has none.<br />----<br /><br />For the victim of anxiety, there is no difference between success and fiasco. His reaction to the one is the same as to the other: both trouble him equally.<br />----<br /><br /><br />I was alone in that cemetery overlooking the village when a pregnant woman came in. I left at once, in order not to look at this corpse-bearer at close range, nor to ruminate upon the contrast between an aggressive womb and the time-worn tombs—between a false promise and the end of all promises.<br />----<br /><br /><br /><br />I am nothing, obviously, but since for so long I wanted to be something, I fail to smother that aspiration, that will: It exists because it has existed, it belabors me and prevails, though I reject it. Try as I do to relegate it to my past, it kicks up again and torments me: never having been satisfied, it has maintained itself Intact, and has no Intention of yielding to my orders. Caught between my will and myself, what can I do?<br />----<br /><br /><br /><br />I pride myself on my capacity to perceive the transitory character of everything. An odd gift which has spoiled all my joys; better: all my sensations.<br />----<br /><br /><br /><br />Getting up with my head full of plans, I would be working, I was sure of it, all morning long. No sooner had I sat down at my desk than the odious, vile, and persuasive refrain: “What do you expect of this world?” stopped me short. And I returned, as usual, to my bed with the hope of finding some answer, of going back to sleep.<br />----<br /><br /><br /><br />Trees are massacred, houses go up—faces, faces everywhere. Man is spreading. Man is the cancer of the earth.<br />----<br /><br /><br /><br />An obsession with the precarious accompanies me in every circumstance: mailing a letter this morning, I told myself it was addressed to a mortal.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5751718442548539329" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2725105278" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10143901742587795723">1-Existence = Torment<br/><br/>2-A book is a postponed suicide.<br/><br/>3-“It is not worth the bother of killing yourself, since you always kill yourself too late”<br/><br/>4-What do you do from morning to night?""I endure myself.”<br/><br/>5- “When people come to me saying they want to kill themselves, I tell them, “What’s your rush? You can kill yourself any time you like. So calm down. Suicide is a positive act.” And they do calm down.”<br/><br/>6- “What I know at sixty, I knew as well at twenty. Forty years of a long, a superfluous, labor </span>
  <span id="freeText10143901742587795723" style="display:none">1-Existence = Torment<br /><br />2-A book is a postponed suicide.<br /><br />3-“It is not worth the bother of killing yourself, since you always kill yourself too late”<br /><br />4-What do you do from morning to night?""I endure myself.”<br /><br />5- “When people come to me saying they want to kill themselves, I tell them, “What’s your rush? You can kill yourself any time you like. So calm down. Suicide is a positive act.” And they do calm down.”<br /><br />6- “What I know at sixty, I knew as well at twenty. Forty years of a long, a superfluous, labor of verification.”<br /><br />7-Every friendship is an inconspicuous drama, a series of subtle wounds<br /><br />8-The more you live, the less useful it seems to have lived.<br /><br />9- Not the slightest trace of reality anywhere—except in my sensations of<br />unreality.<br /><br />10- Man accepts death but not the hour of his death. To die any time, except when one has to die<br /><br />11- No one has been so convinced as I of the futility of everything; and no one has taken so tragically so many futile things<br /><br />12- Only one thing matters: learning to be the loser<br /><br />13- Each generation lives in the absolute: it behaves as if it had reached the apex if not the end of history<br /><br />14- The unfortunate thing about public misfortunes is that everyone regards<br />himself as qualified to talk about them<br /><br />15- The right to suppress everyone that bothers us should rank first in the<br />constitution of the ideal State.<br /><br />16- The only thing the young should be taught is that there is virtually nothing to be hoped for from life. One dreams of a Catalogue of Disappointments which would include all the disillusionments reserved for each and every one of us,to be posted in the schools<br /><br />17- Every exhausted civilization awaits its barbarian, and every barbarian awaits his demon.<br /><br />18- The West: a sweet-smelling rottenness, a perfumed corpse<br /><br />19- We have convictions only if we have studied nothing thoroughly.<br /><br />20-Deep inside, each man feels—and believes—himself to be immortal, even if he knows he will perish the next moment<br /><br />21- We make choices, decisions, as long as we keep to the surface of things; once we reach the depths, we can neither choose nor decide, we can do nothing but regret the surface<br /><br />22- When someone complains that his life has come to nothing, we need merely remind him that life itself is in an analogous situation, if not worse.<br /><br />23- In art and in everything, the commentator is generally better informed and more lucid than the subject of commentary. This is the advantage the murderer has over his victim.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1511292516" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1511292516" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28272535-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575142571p2/28272535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1511292516?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/28272535-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1511292516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10804102102995618811">For the uninitiated: this dude grew up in effin' Transylvania, played soccer with human skulls as a child, chose to live much of his adult life in isolation, and had such an unrelentingly bleak worldview that his once-close friend <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1433597.Samuel_Beckett' title='Samuel Beckett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Samuel Beckett</a> grew exhausted with his pessimism.<br/><br/>In other words, I want to dig him up and marry him.<br/><br/>Really, though, much of his work is incredibly funny. I'd imagine you need to have a few screws loose to really <i>get</i> his sense of humor, but trust me, he's totally got th</span>
  <span id="freeText10804102102995618811" style="display:none">For the uninitiated: this dude grew up in effin' Transylvania, played soccer with human skulls as a child, chose to live much of his adult life in isolation, and had such an unrelentingly bleak worldview that his once-close friend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1433597.Samuel_Beckett" title="Samuel Beckett" rel="nofollow noopener">Samuel Beckett</a> grew exhausted with his pessimism.<br /><br />In other words, I want to dig him up and marry him.<br /><br />Really, though, much of his work is incredibly funny. I'd imagine you need to have a few screws loose to really <i>get</i> his sense of humor, but trust me, he's totally got that whistling-into-the-void thing down to a science. Or, if you don't trust me, just read a few of his aphorisms:<br /><br />"My vision of the future is so <i>exact</i> that if I had children, I should strangle them here and now."<br /><br />"Sometimes I wish I were a cannibal - less for the pleasure of eating someone than for the pleasure of vomiting him."<br /><br />"Having always lived in fear of being surprised by the worst, I have tried in every circumstance to get a head start, flinging myself into misfortune long before it occurred."<br /><br />"If I have been able to hold out till now, it is because each blow, which seemed intolerable at the time, was followed by a second which was worse, then a third, and so on. If I were in hell, I'd want its circles to multiply, in order to count on a new ordeal, more trying than its predecessor. A salutary policy, with regard to torments at least."</span>
  <a data-text-id="10804102102995618811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_213144164" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/213144164" />
    <a title="Charles" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3269182-charles"><img alt="Charles" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1265745471p2/3269182.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/213144164?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles" href="/user/show/3269182-charles">Charles</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213144164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15383241766689541408">Wow! He is quite the philosopher. Through a series of aphorisms he looks at the illusions we all share about life. While most of what he writes looks like a negative spin on living, the principle theme seems to be that there is truth beyond the illusions if only we dare to penetrate it. I especially liked his idea that "joy" represented the "one true victory over the world." Read it if you dare. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

And what has made my life endurable to me is the curiosity as to how I would get from one minute, one day, one year to the next."<br/><br/>Strangely consoling, Cioran's piercing, eloquent, and thought-provoking exploration of the metaphysically complex relationship between life and death, between being and non-being, leaves you in a state of melancholic, yet sweet tearfulness. </span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1876169367570388915">The giddy, idiotic rush and a momentary intimation of a smile when you feel, at long last, understood.<br/>Yet this relation to somebody dead to history means nothing. Of what value or worth is this confirmation?<br/><br/>Finally reading this man who was constantly tortured by his sleeplessness, and relentlessly pursued like a cast shadow follows its host by the thought of suicide. That he did not succumb and suffered through existence until his time impresses me greatly. The idea of suicide, that an escape fr</span>
  <span id="freeText1876169367570388915" style="display:none">The giddy, idiotic rush and a momentary intimation of a smile when you feel, at long last, understood.<br />Yet this relation to somebody dead to history means nothing. Of what value or worth is this confirmation?<br /><br />Finally reading this man who was constantly tortured by his sleeplessness, and relentlessly pursued like a cast shadow follows its host by the thought of suicide. That he did not succumb and suffered through existence until his time impresses me greatly. The idea of suicide, that an escape from being alive was eternally available, seemed a comfort to him, he who writes in this book, "It's not worth the bother of killing yourself, since you always kill yourself too late," yet he opted to suffer through every boring and dreadful moment, through the sheer boredom of being alive. <br />Was it Montaigne who wrote that philsophy is a preparation for death? In no way is this more thoroughly confirmed in the writings of Emil Cioran who wrote not one sentence that is not without a recognition of death.<br /><br />Cioran, so far, has been keeping me ideological company in thoughts of the ineradicable relativity of the world, the humorous absurdity of being thrown into existence and bearing it needlessly therefrom, and the utterly contradictory and self-defeating nature of the self.<br />Would Cioran not agree that man is an irrational animal?</span>
  <a data-text-id="1876169367570388915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2165220618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17506538241705725023">Reading this was an unusual experience.<br/>On the one hand, I agree with many of Cioran’s observations. On the other, he takes some way too far and comes off as an emo teenager smoking clove cigarettes and feeling misunderstood. What is most hilarious is the thought that someone with these extreme feelings and thoughts would also put in the effort to write them down and publish. That seems contradictory.<br/>It reminds me of geeks and hipsters. Geeks honestly like the things they like, and if it happens </span>
  <span id="freeText17506538241705725023" style="display:none">Reading this was an unusual experience.<br />On the one hand, I agree with many of Cioran’s observations. On the other, he takes some way too far and comes off as an emo teenager smoking clove cigarettes and feeling misunderstood. What is most hilarious is the thought that someone with these extreme feelings and thoughts would also put in the effort to write them down and publish. That seems contradictory.<br />It reminds me of geeks and hipsters. Geeks honestly like the things they like, and if it happens to be momentarily popular then they can be confused with hipsters. But hipsters would never express genuine enthusiasm for things once they aren’t popular anymore, or would eschew the idea that they like something that is popular. It is about the appearance.<br />So, if Cioran really thought all of this was pointless and as dark as he writes, why would he write it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="17506538241705725023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Ilya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28772979-ilya"><img alt="Ilya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1437928967p2/28772979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1608738008?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ilya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ilya" href="/user/show/28772979-ilya">Ilya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28772979-ilya?shelf=loss">loss</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28772979-ilya?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1608738008" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12756201803003696803">Emil Cioran must have been a blast at cocktail parties! If someone had asked him, over a drink, "What do you do from morning to night?" Cioran would reply: "I endure myself." All jokes aside I generally disagree with Cioran's hopeless outlook on daily existence. While it is very likely that, in the grand scheme of things, our existence is meaningless, I find it even more meaningless to not try to make something out of your life. There is no hope in Cioran's rhetoric. I could only imagine how mis</span>
  <span id="freeText12756201803003696803" style="display:none">Emil Cioran must have been a blast at cocktail parties! If someone had asked him, over a drink, "What do you do from morning to night?" Cioran would reply: "I endure myself." All jokes aside I generally disagree with Cioran's hopeless outlook on daily existence. While it is very likely that, in the grand scheme of things, our existence is meaningless, I find it even more meaningless to not try to make something out of your life. There is no hope in Cioran's rhetoric. I could only imagine how miserable his daily existence really was. That being said, it's not just hopeless rhetoric page after page; there is a lot of interesting ideas here. The idea about salvation being escaping from "the kingdom of consciousness" really stood out to me. 3 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12756201803003696803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jessica Sullivan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1246157-jessica-sullivan"><img alt="Jessica Sullivan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526472411p2/1246157.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1177812819?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica Sullivan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica Sullivan" href="/user/show/1246157-jessica-sullivan">Jessica Sullivan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1246157-jessica-sullivan?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1246157-jessica-sullivan?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1177812819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11612067453448968271">Strangely enough, this was a joy to read. Cioran balances his extreme nihilism and pessimism with wicked humor and irony. Kind of like if Rust Cohle had more self-doubt and a better sense of humor. To paraphrase an Amazon review I came across, there's something oddly life-affirming about his gleefully pessimistic meditations on death, decay, nihilism, and Buddhism. Also worth mentioning: he writes beautifully. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3024754859" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer531193745004071837">Huh, Y'all , this is the one not to miss in reading Cioran works and the one which profoundly fought the battles he faced through the lassitude of being born. I think he also got troublesome of remorseful life but still active to fight till the death along with the rare Incomprehensible Philosophical jargons. xD<br/>At all, big thanks for mentioning the epic trio of Aristotle-Aquinas-Hegel idealism of all time.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('GgCs3d3G8gaxiMTS4C7+FRH1sY+A7mZ0BswlVP86nEcR+tqCQM8EaIcRZpnqkHxyJs96BpEPjZcA9r2Z1yP8wg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

It is without question Schopenhauer\'s greatest work. Conceived and published before the philosopher was 30 and expanded 25 years later, it is the summation of a lifetime of thought.\n\nFor 70 years, the only unabridged English translation of this work was the Haldane-Kemp collaboration. In 1958, a new translation by E. F. J. Payne appeared that decisively supplanted the older one. Payne\'s translation is superior because it corrects nearly 1,000 errors and omissions in the Haldane-Kemp translation, and it is based on the definitive 1937 German edition of Schopenhauer\'s work prepared by Dr. Arthur Hübscher. Payne\'s edition is the first to translate into English the text\'s many quotations in half a dozen languages. The unsta<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16660314726637351323\" style=\"display:none\">It was to correct common misconceptions about his thought that Jean-Paul Sartre, the most dominent European intellectual of the post-World War II decades, accepted an invitation to speak on October 29, 1945, at the Club Maintenant in Paris. The unstated objective of his lecture (“Existentialism Is a Humanism”) was to expound his philosophy as a form of “existentialism,” a term much bandied about at the time. Sartre asserted that existentialism was essentially a doctrine for philosophers, though, ironically, he was about to make it accessible to a general audience. The published text of his lecture quickly became one of the bibles of existentialism and made Sartre an international celebrity.\n\nThe idea of freedom occupies the center of Sartre’s doctrine. Man, born into an empty, godless universe, is nothing to begin with. He creates his essence—his self, his being—through the choices he freely makes (“existence precedes essence”). Were it not for the contingency of his death, he would never end. Choosing to be this or that is to affirm the value of what we choose. In choosing, therefore, we commit not only ourselves but all of mankind.\n\nThis book presents a new English translation of Sartre’s 1945 lecture and his analysis of Camus’s The Stranger, along with a discussion of these works by acclaimed Sartre biographer Annie Cohen-Solal. This edition is a translation of the 1996 French edition, which includes Arlette Elkaïm-Sartre’s introduction and a Q&amp;A with Sartre about his lecture.<\/span>\n  <a Presenting itself as an investigation into the suicide of a close friend—perhaps real, perhaps fictional—more than twenty years earlier, Levé gives us, little by little, a striking portrait of a man, with all his talents and flaws, who chose to reject his life, and all the people who loved him, in favor of oblivion. Gradually, through Levé’s casually obsessive, pointillist, beautiful ruminations, we come to know a stoic, sensible, thoughtful man who bears more than a slight psychological resemblance to Levé himself. But Suicide is more than just a compendium of memories of an old friend; it is a near-exhaustive catalog of the ramifications and effects of the act of suicide, and a unique and melancholy farewell to       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/68189.Emil_M_Cioran">About Emil M. Cioran</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/68189.Emil_M_Cioran"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1449686091p3/68189.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/68189.Emil_M_Cioran">Emil M. Cioran
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,759
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10051914473509621699">Born in 1911 in Rășinari, a small village in the Carpathian Mountains of Romania, raised under the rule of a father who was a Romanian Orthodox priest and a mother who was prone to depression, Emil Cioran wrote his first five books in Romanian. Some of these are collections of brief essays (one or two pages, on average); others are collections of aphorisms. Suffering from insomnia since his adoles</span>
  <span id="freeText10051914473509621699" style="display:none">Born in 1911 in Rășinari, a small village in the Carpathian Mountains of Romania, raised under the rule of a father who was a Romanian Orthodox priest and a mother who was prone to depression, Emil Cioran wrote his first five books in Romanian. Some of these are collections of brief essays (one or two pages, on average); others are collections of aphorisms. Suffering from insomnia since his adolescent years in Sibiu, the young Cioran studied philosophy in the “little Paris” of Bucarest.<br />A prolific publicist, he became a well-known figure, along with Mircea Eliade, Constantin Noïca, and his future close friend Eugene Ionesco (with whom he shared the Royal Foundation’s Young Writers Prize in 1934 for his first book, On the Heights of Despair). <br /><br />Influenced by the German romantics, by Schopenhauer, Nietzsche, and the Lebensphilosophie of Schelling and Bergson, by certain Russian writers, including Chestov, Rozanov, and Dostoyevsky, and by the Romanian poet Eminescu, Cioran wrote lyrical and expansive meditations that were often metaphysical in nature and whose recurrent themes were death, despair, solitude, history, music, saintliness and the mystics (cf. Tears and Saints, 1937) – all of which are themes that one finds again in his French writings. In his highly controversial book, The Transfiguration of Romania (1937), Cioran, who was at that time close to the Romanian fascists, violently criticized his country and his compatriots on the basis of a contrast between such “little nations” as Romania, which were contemptible from the perspective of universal history and great nations, such as France or Germany, which took their destiny into their own hands. <br /><br />After spending two years in Germany, Cioran arrived in Paris in 1936. He continued to write in Romanian until the early 1940s (he wrote his last article in Romanian in 1943, which is also the year in which he began writing in French). The break with Romanian became definitive in 1946, when, in the course of translating Mallarmé, he suddenly decided to give up his native tongue since no one spoke it in Paris. He then began writing in French a book that, thanks to numerous intensive revisions, would eventually become the impressive 'A Short History of Decay' (1949) -- the first of a series of ten books in which Cioran would continue to explore his perennial obsessions, with a growing detachment that allies him equally with the Greek sophists, the French moralists, and the oriental sages. He wrote existential vituperations and other destructive reflections in a classical French style that he felt was diametrically opposed to the looseness of his native Romanian; he described it as being like a “straight-jacket” that required him to control his temperamental excesses and his lyrical flights. The books in which he expressed his radical disillusionment appeared, with decreasing frequency, over a period of more than three decades, during which time he shared his solitude with his companion Simone Boué in a miniscule garret in the center of Paris, where he lived as a spectator more and more turned in on himself and maintaining an ever greater distance from a world that he rejected as much on the historical level (History and Utopia, 1960) as on the ontological (The Fall into Time, 1964), raising his misanthropy to heights of subtlety (The Trouble with being Born, 1973), while also allowing to appear from time to time a humanism composed of irony, bitterness, and preciosity (Exercices d’admiration, 1986, and the posthumously published Notebooks). <br /><br />Denied the right to return to Romania during the years of the communist regime, and attracting international attention only late in his career, Cioran died in Paris in 1995. <br /><br />Nicolas Cavaillès <br />Translated by Thomas Cousineau</span>
  <a data-text-id="10051914473509621699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
